## Domain Knowledge
**Domain**: classical mechanics (nonlinear oscillations / dynamical systems)
**Analysis**: The target dv_dt is acceleration, and the available state variables (x, v) with time t match second-order oscillator dynamics written as a first-order system. Nonlinear harmonic oscillators typically model dv_dt as a sum of restoring forces (functions of x), damping forces (functions of v and sometimes x), and optional external/parametric forcing (functions of t).

**Common formula structures** (built-in generic + LLM domain-specific):
- Arrhenius equation: k = c0 * exp(c1 / T) (chemical reaction rate dependence on temperature)
- Coulomb's / Newton's inverse-square law: F = c0 / r**2 (gravitational or electrostatic force)
- Stefan-Boltzmann law: P = c0 * T**4 (blackbody radiation power vs temperature)
- Beer-Lambert law: I = c0 * exp(c1 * x) (light intensity attenuation through a medium)
- Michaelis-Menten kinetics: v = c0 * S / (S + c1) (enzyme-catalyzed reaction saturation)
- Hill equation: y = c0 * x**c1 / (x**c1 + c2) (cooperative binding, sigmoidal dose-response)
- Kepler's third law: T**2 = c0 * a**3 (orbital period vs semi-major axis)
- Newton's law of cooling: T = c0 + c1 * exp(c2 * t) (exponential thermal relaxation)
- Clausius-Clapeyron relation: log(P) = c0 + c1 / T (vapor pressure vs temperature)
- Langmuir adsorption isotherm: theta = c0 * P / (1 + c1 * P) (surface adsorption equilibrium)
- Freundlich isotherm: q = c0 * C**c1 (empirical adsorption on heterogeneous surface)
- Stokes' drag: F = c0 * r * v (viscous drag force on a sphere in fluid)
- Logistic growth: N = c0 / (1 + c1 * exp(c2 * t)) (population dynamics with carrying capacity)
- Planck-Wien approximation: I = c0 * f**3 * exp(c1 * f / T) (thermal radiation spectrum)
- Simple harmonic oscillator (Hooke restoring): dv_dt = -c0 * x (undamped linear oscillator)
- Viscously damped oscillator: dv_dt = -c0 * x - c1 * v (linear damping to equilibrium)
- Time-varying/parametric stiffness: dv_dt = -(c0 + c1 * cos(c2 * t)) * x - c3 * v (parametric excitation / Mathieu-type)
- Driven damped oscillator: dv_dt = -c0 * x - c1 * v + c2 * cos(c3 * t) (external periodic forcing)
- Duffing oscillator (cubic stiffness): dv_dt = -c0 * x - c1 * x^3 - c2 * v (hardening/softening spring nonlinearity)
- Duffing with drive: dv_dt = -c0 * x - c1 * x^3 - c2 * v + c3 * cos(c4 * t) (chaos/large-amplitude response)
- Van der Pol oscillator: dv_dt = c0 * (c1 - x^2) * v - c2 * x (self-excited oscillations / limit cycle)
- Rayleigh oscillator (nonlinear damping in v): dv_dt = -c0 * x + (c1 - c2 * v^2) * v (self-excitation via velocity-dependent damping)
- Nonlinear (quadratic) drag damping: dv_dt = -c0 * x - c1 * Abs(v) * v (aerodynamic-like dissipation)
- Coulomb/friction-like damping: dv_dt = -c0 * x - c1 * tanh(c2 * v) (dry friction regularized; near-constant friction force)
- Nonlinear restoring via odd polynomial series: dv_dt = -(c0 * x + c1 * x^3 + c2 * x^5) - c3 * v (general conservative stiffness expansion)
- Potential-gradient form (conservative + damping): dv_dt = -(c0 * x + c1 * x^3) - c2 * v (interpretable as dv_dt = -dV/dx - damping with V(x) ~ x^2 + x^4)

**Formula structure heuristic features**:
- Oscillation damping envelope → Exponential decay envelope f(x)*exp(-c*Abs(x)) for symmetric damped oscillation, Gaussian envelope f(x)*exp(-c*x**2) for localized wave packets
- Saturation/clipping effects → Rational fraction x/(1+x), tanh; for variables with alternating positive/negative in oscillatory systems, selectively apply Abs
- Growth/decay → Exponential growth exp(c*t), Logistic 1/(1+exp(-c*x)), power law x**c
- Threshold/activation behavior → Max(x-c, 0), piecewise functions
- Coupling/cross effects → Product terms between variables x*y, power-law coupling x**c1 * y**c2
- Restoring-force symmetry: if the equilibrium is at x = 0 and physics is symmetric, dv_dt often contains odd functions of x (x, x^3, x^5), not even powers alone.
- Conservative vs dissipative split: terms depending only on x act like conservative restoring forces, while terms depending on v (e.g., -c*v, -c*Abs(v)*v) represent dissipation or injection of energy.
- Limit-cycle self-excitation signature: factors like (c - x^2)*v or (c - v^2)*v indicate negative damping at small amplitude and positive damping at large amplitude, producing stable oscillations.
- Amplitude-dependent frequency indicator: adding x^3 (Duffing) makes oscillation frequency depend on amplitude; the sign of the cubic term distinguishes hardening vs softening behavior.
- External periodic forcing: additive sin/cos terms in t (e.g., +c*cos(c*t)) suggest driven resonance and possible entrainment/chaos when combined with nonlinearity.
- Parametric resonance indicator: a time-periodic coefficient multiplying x (e.g., -(c0 + c1*cos(c2*t))*x) suggests Mathieu-type instability tongues rather than simple forcing response.
- Dry-friction behavior: tanh(c*v) or sign-like dependence on v implies nearly constant-magnitude opposing force and can create stick-slip or nonsmooth dynamics.
- Nonlinear drag regime: Abs(v)*v indicates dissipation scaling with v^2 and strong damping at high speeds, typical of turbulent/aerodynamic drag.
- Energy-balance sanity check: purely restoring terms should point toward equilibrium (dv_dt opposite sign of x for small x), while damping terms should oppose motion (negative power ~ v*dv_dt contribution).

1. `x` (linear)

The key challenge: **sin(v)** cannot be represented by polynomial terms in v (like v**3, v**5, etc.) or by log/exp terms. Also, `Abs(v)**(1/3)` is a specific fractional power of |v|.

Now let me check each candidate for the presence of `sin(v)` or something that can represent it:

**None of the candidates contain sin(v).** They all use polynomial terms (v, v**3), log, exp, power terms like v**c, x**c, t-dependent terms, etc.

Since `sin(v)` is a transcendental function that is linearly independent from all polynomial, exponential, and logarithmic functions, **no polynomial/log/exp combination can exactly equal sin(v)** for all v. The coefficient of sin(v) in the GT is `-0.448061811383339 ≠ 0`, so this term cannot be eliminated.

Let me verify: Could any candidate somehow hide a sin(v)? Looking through all 50 candidates - none contain sin, cos, or any trigonometric function. They use powers (v**c), log, exp, and polynomial terms only.

By the principle of linear independence of basis functions (Rule 3), since sin(v) cannot be expressed as any combination of polynomials, exponentials, or logarithms, **no candidate can match the GT**.
